<div id="review_1651471239" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1651471239" />
    <a title="Luke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19706661-luke"><img alt="Luke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483200709p2/19706661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1651471239?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luke" href="/user/show/19706661-luke">Luke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/24302397-black-boy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19706661-luke?shelf=%C4%81-staking-not-only-my-own-life-a-re">ā-staking-not-only-my-own-life-a-re</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19706661-luke?shelf=blackness-a-reader">blackness-a-reader</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19706661-luke?shelf=books-that-changed-me">books-that-changed-me</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19706661-luke?shelf=a-black-lives-reader">a-black-lives-reader</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19706661-luke?shelf=black-literature">black-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1651471239" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14022601208838060826">What can I say? Black Boy is arguably one of the best (underrated) autobiographies / memoirs ever written. <br/><br/>As an author, political and social commentator and all round citizen of the world, what is there not to love about the great Richard Wright?<br/><br/>I enjoyed this book on both a personal and political level that I'm not sure it's possible for me to break down how I feel about it into this little box, let alone critique it (although I would have loved it to have encompassed Wright's reflections on h</span>
  <span id="freeText14022601208838060826" style="display:none">What can I say? Black Boy is arguably one of the best (underrated) autobiographies / memoirs ever written. <br /><br />As an author, political and social commentator and all round citizen of the world, what is there not to love about the great Richard Wright?<br /><br />I enjoyed this book on both a personal and political level that I'm not sure it's possible for me to break down how I feel about it into this little box, let alone critique it (although I would have loved it to have encompassed Wright's reflections on his later adult life, but that's just me being pernickety). Instead, I think I'll just comment on Wright's standing as a writer and all round human (after all, who likes spoilers anyway?).<br /><br />Ideally I'd prefer not to attempt to compare Wright to any of his contemporaries, or indeed other writers of his time. Having said that, the fact that Wright receives much less attention than many other African American authors should be pointed out (so in that sense, I suppose comparisons are kind of unavoidable). Put simply, as scholars, messrs Baldwin, Malcolm X, MLK Jr (to name three notorious 20th century black American figures) were all great visionaries with fascinating personalities. All wrote influential works (novels, memoirs, speeches), works that have inspired a great deal of people (myself included). Yet none of their output has captivated me in quite the same way as Wright's. <br /><br />This may well have something to do with the fact that Wright's persona (his lived experience, as imbibed in his works) is one that is both unassuming and extraordinary. Ultimately, the sheer quality and accessibility of Wright's prose - coupled with his enduring humility despite intense adversity - are what set him apart from his successors and contemporaries, and this shows through in Black Boy.<br /><br />Again, this is just my view. I'd love to know how others feel about Wright!<br /><br />Whatever you do though, read this book for its humanity but most of all, its perspicacity. <br /><br />P.S. It took me the best part of a year to finish this book, and I'm so glad I took my time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14022601208838060826" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6714078067621846412">I loved this book, and thought it was nearly perfect up until the last part when he started flirting with the Communist party in Chicago. I just felt there was a jarring disconnect, because he was being so heartfelt and honest about his personal experiences with discrimination and coming of age in a divided America, that the Communist part while fascinating, just felt like it belonged in a different book.<br/><br/>On Communism:<br/>"I knew, as I watched, that I was looking at the future of mankind, that this w</span>
  <span id="freeText6714078067621846412" style="display:none">I loved this book, and thought it was nearly perfect up until the last part when he started flirting with the Communist party in Chicago. I just felt there was a jarring disconnect, because he was being so heartfelt and honest about his personal experiences with discrimination and coming of age in a divided America, that the Communist part while fascinating, just felt like it belonged in a different book.<br /><br />On Communism:<br />"I knew, as I watched, that I was looking at the future of mankind, that this way of living would finally win out. I knew that in no other way could the emotional capacities, the passionate nature of men be so deeply tapped. In no other system yet devised could man so clearly reveal his destiny on earth, a destiny to rise and grapple with the world in which he lives, to wring from it the satisfactions he feels he must have."<br /><br />On the problems of America and race relations:<br />"I felt that the Negro could not live a full, human life under the conditions imposed upon him by America; and I felt, too, that America, for different reasons, could not live a full, human life. It seemed to me, then, that if the Negro solved his problem, he would be solving infinitely more than his problem alone. I felt certain that the Negro could never solve his problem until the deeper problem of American civilization had been faced and solved. And because the Negro was the most cast-out of all the outcast people in America, I felt that no other group in America could tackle this problem of what our American lives meant so well as the Negro could."<br /><br />But on a more personal level, Wright describes the crippling racism he felt growing up in early 20th century America:<br />"I had never in my life been abused by whites, but I had already become as conditioned to their existence as though I had been the victim of a thousand lynchings".<br />and<br />"I did not suspect that I would never get intimately into their lives, that I was doomed to live with them but not of them, that I had my own strange and separate road, a road which in later years would make them wonder how I had come to tread it".<br /><br />This was a beautiful, fascinating, and poignant work and like many others reviewing it here on Goodreads, I'm reading it as book one of the free Open Yale online course on the American Novel since 1945.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_843965323" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/843965323" />
    <a title="Christopher Trader" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1816075-christopher-trader"><img alt="Christopher Trader" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1230402414p2/1816075.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/843965323?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher Trader" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher Trader" href="/user/show/1816075-christopher-trader">Christopher Trader</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">EVERYONE</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer843965323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3245565991243374876">I must admit, right from the start, that this will be a very poor review. It will be a poor review because such a work of wonder and artistry could never be properly praised with mere words alone. I fear I lack the talent to even come near doing justice to the education this book has given me. To the depth of philosophy and emotion drawn forth by Wright's writing. <br/><br/>I want to start by sharing why I chose to read this work. Many years ago I purchased a copy of Wright's work, Native Son. I don't rem</span>
  <span id="freeText3245565991243374876" style="display:none">I must admit, right from the start, that this will be a very poor review. It will be a poor review because such a work of wonder and artistry could never be properly praised with mere words alone. I fear I lack the talent to even come near doing justice to the education this book has given me. To the depth of philosophy and emotion drawn forth by Wright's writing. <br /><br />I want to start by sharing why I chose to read this work. Many years ago I purchased a copy of Wright's work, Native Son. I don't remember what led me to it, but nevertheless I read it and fell madly in love with Wright's talent as a story-crafter and writer. I was floored both with his talent and with the content of the story, but this is not a review of Native Son, so I will digress. <br /><br />After reading Native Son and being absolutely amazed with Wright's talents, I wanted to read more by him, including this book, Black Boy, his Autobiography. I found it to be one of the most grammatically soothing books I have ever read, though it was a tough read emotionally at times. Wright goes into detail in this book about crafting perfect sentences, about his desire as a writer to formulate his words in a detailed but pleasing manner. I believe he really understated this in it's description, but his writing itself shows the truth of it.<br /><br />Every sentence, paragraph, and even word is carefully chosen by Wright as he composes. This is not Literature, but perfected prose. When you read Wright you don't hit dry patches that make you long for the next section of the work. You don't feel that you are performing a task in reading his works, instead you feel as though you are reading the finest poetry from the comfort of a blanket in the middle of the most beautiful park you've ever seen, on the most perfect summer day you could ever imagine. <br /><br />Aside from the writing itself, the content of this book is stunning in it's detail and magnitude. Wright manages not just to describe situations in his life to you, but to place you into those situations, to give you a glimpse of feeling what it is that he felt. I find this especially important for the White reader in America. Many white's feel as though we are sensitive to African American issues and understand their culture, but I tell you here that you will admit to yourself after reading this book that you truly had not one half of the understanding that you thought you did. <br /><br />The book is composed of two major sections, the first being Wright's upbringing in the South, and the latter half detailing his young adult life in the North (Chicago). We learn, deeply, the struggles of growing up in the South as an African American. The pure degradation, fear, hunger, and angst of it all. Wright also goes into much detail of the suffering he withstood from being raised in a deeply religious home, and the poison and division this can create amongst a family. <br /><br />Once in the North we learn how differently interactions are between White and Black vs. how they are in the South, but are also shown the inner fear and paranoia that can make life just as dreadful as it is in the South. <br /><br />***SMALL SPOILERS BELOW***<br /><br />The overall content of the book details Wright's learning to read at a young age, his hunger and desire to use this ability, and its eventually leading to his desire to write. The other major events cover Wright's dealings with the Communist Party of the U.S. and their eventual betrayal of him. <br /><br />For Leftist readers there is a lot of eye-opening here. We are shown the inner-workings of a leftist political party, for good or bad. It was most interesting to me because I had no idea Wright was involved with the CPUSA, and being a staunch Leftist myself it was almost serendipitous to find this being covered in a book that I had not sought out for this reason. Without going into too much detail, I just want to note that I not only suggest this as reading for ALL white's in America, but especially for any and every person with a Leftist bent. <br /><br />Overall my head is spinning after reading this book. There is so much to think about and process. I'll probably read the book again just to take notes and give the book the deep analysis that it truly deserves. Wright is one of the smartest and most eloquent writers I have ever come across, and this book is a prime example of this. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3245565991243374876" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_694101651" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/694101651" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/694101651?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Aubrey by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/1652898-karen">·Karen·</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=z-2">z-2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=pure-power-of-gr">pure-power-of-gr</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2017">r-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=authorness">authorness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-everything">person-of-everything</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer694101651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8289610556631080535"><blockquote>
  <i>Whenever my environment had failed to support or nourish me, I had clutched at books; consequently, my belief in books had risen more out of a sense of desperation than from any abiding conviction of their absolute value.</i>
</blockquote>If you've found your way to this corner of the Internet, the above quote should look strangely familiar. It's the most popular quote accredited to Richard Wright, and considering the context (as if that were not mandated for every critical engagement), this tells us a great </span>
  <span id="freeText8289610556631080535" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>Whenever my environment had failed to support or nourish me, I had clutched at books; consequently, my belief in books had risen more out of a sense of desperation than from any abiding conviction of their absolute value.</i>
</blockquote>If you've found your way to this corner of the Internet, the above quote should look strangely familiar. It's the most popular quote accredited to Richard Wright, and considering the context (as if that were not mandated for every critical engagement), this tells us a great deal. For one, this sentence appears only in the amputated version of <i>Black Boy</i>, which contains only the first and Southern half of Wright's autobiography due to the predilections of his Northern sponsors for the nice and the neat. The work wasn't reconstituted in full until after its author's death in 1960, the second part published separately in 1977 and the two not combined until 1991, the year of my birth and nearly half a century after the original publication date. If you read the fully spliced together 1998 edition of mine, you won't find this quote in the main text itself, but buried in the end notes. You'll also notice that the quote itself has undergone some amputation itself, attested accurately to, of course, with the end dots, but providing such a holistically apolitical statement that few would feel that adding anything to it would improve its bite-sized value. Such is history, and aesthetics, and public consumption, but none can obfuscate the truth.<blockquote>
  <i>In my dealing with whites I was conscious of the entirety of my relations with them, and they were conscious only of what was happening at a given moment.<br /><br />Gambling had never appealed to me. I could not conceive of any game holding more risks than the life I was living.</i>
</blockquote>What would Richard Wright have been, had he not been a writer? He mopped floors in hospitals, ran errands for eye glass polishers, cashiered movie tickets, sold insurance policies, swept streets, sorted mail, alongside developing interests in psychology, politics, self-actualization, the relationship between individual and the masses, individual and history, individual and the hierarchy, race, communism, the lot. That top often truncated quote, caught as it is in the unusual cross purposes of history as the conclusion that the passing of time has erased the necessity for, attests that literature may not have been the first, or second, or even fifteenth choice had Wright not had every other rug pulled out from underneath him and hanged on the nearest branch. That quote does not attest to the fulfillment of the hierarchy of needs, but the desperation of gnawing off a limb caught in a trap, and the fact that it fits nicely into the solipsistic holier-than-thou self-entitlement that so often plagues the partakes of literature is pure coincidence.<blockquote>
  <i>He was white, and I could never do to him what he and his kind had done to me. Therefore, I reasoned, stealing was not a violation of my ethics, but of his; I felt that things were rigged in his favor and any action I took to circumvent his scheme of life was justified.<br /><br />We were toying with the idea of death for no reason that stemmed from our own lives, but because the men who ruled us had thrust the idea into our minds.</i>
</blockquote>I've seen comments that not all contained with this book is nonfiction with regards to Wright's individual life. For one, frankly, I don't care, because if Wright was completely unaffected by collective and/or transgenerational trauma, I'll eat my hat, and that shit will fuck with your memory like nothing else. Secondly, unless you only read nonfiction written by Siri and the like, it's merely a different breed of subjective when compared to so called fiction, and the insertion Wright practices is equivalent in weight to every smoothing over or politically wise erasure or any other childification of material for the sake of privacy, efficacy, or shame. Thirdly, what other black person during Wright's day and age gained such a measure of credible exposure as to put their life and experiences on public broadcast and garner such a positive and world wide effect? I won't claim that Wright gave every person who contributed, unknowingly or otherwise, their own experiences to his autobiography their socioeconomic due, but I would trust him to do so far more than I would others who haven't had the meaning of life and liberty and money beaten into them so often for so long.<blockquote>
  <i><b>1947</b> - Refuses offer from Hollywood producer to film <u>Native Son</u> with character Bigger Thomas changed to a white man.<br /><br />To permit the birth of this new consciousness in these people was proof that those who ruled it not quite know what they were doing, assuming that they were trying to save themselves and their class. Had they understood what was happening, they would never have allowed millions of perplexed and defeated people to sit together for long hours and talk, for out of their talk was rising a new realization of life. And once this new conception of themselves had formed, no power on earth could alter it.</i>
</blockquote>The most heartbreaking thing for me was not the endless train of tortures Wright faced in the South, or the baffled antipathy he had to confront in the North, but when his dream of Communism died for good. Part II's title, 'The Horror and the Glory', comes not from any observation of the general relationship between black people and whites, but from witnessing a trial conducted by the Communist Party of one of their members, which is full of hope as it is of miscommunication, ideological myopia, inherited fear, and the safety of conforming numbers trumping real connections between human beings. Coming as this does in the midst of rising Fascism and a looming world war, it is heartbreaking to look back at how what could have been was torn apart not only by the underhanded sabotage of the FBI and CIA, but by the very individual souls straining to connect in the first place. Here in the future, communism has regrouped somewhat from the days of the McCarthy period, but I'm not involved enough with ongoing discussions of its intersection with race and gender and sexuality to see what has changed and what has not, what has been lost and what has been gained. I don't think Wright would have lived to this day even without being condemned to chronic malnutrition and psychological terror (you could just feed the kids in your backyard instead of constantly psychologically distancing yourself overseas, cause I guarantee that your "Third World" label can aptly be applied to large swathes of this capitalistic hellscape that you currently call home), but I still want to know what he would have thought.<blockquote>
  <i>My life as a Negro in America had led me to feel—though my helplessness had made me try to hide it from myself—that the problem of human unity was more important than bread, more important than physical living itself; for I felt that without a common bond uniting men, without a continuous current of shared thought and feeling circulating through the social system, like blood coursing through the body, there could be no living worthy of being called human.</i>
</blockquote>The notes confirm Wright was pretty much right every time he avoided doing something out of paranoia that he was being tailed by the genocidal antiblackness of his home country. How fucked is that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8289610556631080535" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36459924-irene?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36459924-irene?shelf=race-and-racism">race-and-racism</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1895005399" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17356907662520386781">Native Son was assigned in one of my university courses, and I remember it was an electrifying experience for me. I remember doing coursework at a local Starbucks and feeling like I could not put down the book. Of course, we never discussed it fully during our course and I ended up buying <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/228630.Black_Boy' title='Black Boy by Richard Wright' rel='nofollow noopener'>Black Boy</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/54761.Uncle_Tom_s_Children' title='Uncle Tom's Children by Richard Wright' rel='nofollow noopener'>Uncle Tom's Children</a>. Recently, I had to organize my book collection at my family home and came across the Harper Perennial Centennial edition of Black Boy.<br/><br/>I read it these past two weeks and the s</span>
  <span id="freeText17356907662520386781" style="display:none">Native Son was assigned in one of my university courses, and I remember it was an electrifying experience for me. I remember doing coursework at a local Starbucks and feeling like I could not put down the book. Of course, we never discussed it fully during our course and I ended up buying <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/228630.Black_Boy" title="Black Boy by Richard Wright" rel="nofollow noopener">Black Boy</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/54761.Uncle_Tom_s_Children" title="Uncle Tom's Children by Richard Wright" rel="nofollow noopener">Uncle Tom's Children</a>. Recently, I had to organize my book collection at my family home and came across the Harper Perennial Centennial edition of Black Boy.<br /><br />I read it these past two weeks and the scenes and the images took a strong hold in my mind that I became very unsettled and had to take breaks from reading it. From the first scene when Richard accidentally sets fire to the house, to his days of being a drunkard to what he did to the poor kitten, it was very hard to read it. Richard's experiences under Jim Crow South is necessary reading for understanding the black experience in America. This is a groundbreaking memoir. So many despicable scenes and horrifying crimes - it all happened less than 100 years ago. It's just insane! <br /><br />Richard shows us a part of life and a group of the disenfranchised and how they live - this is one of the higher purposes of good writing. Richard provides insights into the writing process when he expresses how he learned more about good writing by reading, and his frustrations at making shoddy writing that didn't reflect his ambitions, or when he expresses his desire to write because it was his way of existing, surviving in the world. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17356907662520386781" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12587228694384342588">In his semi-autobiographical novel, Richard Wright brilliantly describes the life of a "black boy" growing up in the racist and hostile south of the early 1900s. Moving north to Chicago, he gravitates to the Communist Party, whose race-blind approach gives him hope - but then becomes entangled in a different kind of repression. <br/><br/>The novel isn't just about cruelty and struggle and hunger; the pages are filled with the wonder and beauty Wright finds in reading, imagination and writing. He ends the </span>
  <span id="freeText12587228694384342588" style="display:none">In his semi-autobiographical novel, Richard Wright brilliantly describes the life of a "black boy" growing up in the racist and hostile south of the early 1900s. Moving north to Chicago, he gravitates to the Communist Party, whose race-blind approach gives him hope - but then becomes entangled in a different kind of repression. <br /><br />The novel isn't just about cruelty and struggle and hunger; the pages are filled with the wonder and beauty Wright finds in reading, imagination and writing. He ends the novel with: "I would hurl words into this darkness and wait for an echo and if an echo sounded, no matter how faintly, I would send other words to tell, to march, to fight, to create a sense of the hunger for life that gnaws in us all, to keep alive in our hearts a sense of the inexpressibly human."</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5302811377835241774">So much to be said about this book that I couldn't possibly give a good review and include everything.<br/><br/>Many times I was left speechless and at times laughed at the innocence of a child who knew no better and was forced to grow up too fast and learn things on his own. Wright was definitely a character and he shares his experience about his family, the many times he had to move and the number of schools he attended because of these moves. He shares his ordeal with hunger, a father who abandons them</span>
  <span id="freeText5302811377835241774" style="display:none">So much to be said about this book that I couldn't possibly give a good review and include everything.<br /><br />Many times I was left speechless and at times laughed at the innocence of a child who knew no better and was forced to grow up too fast and learn things on his own. Wright was definitely a character and he shares his experience about his family, the many times he had to move and the number of schools he attended because of these moves. He shares his ordeal with hunger, a father who abandons them, a mother who struggles to provide for him and his brother.<br /><br />Tragedy, racism, illness, defiance, religion, education are all part of this wonderfully written account of a boy who struggled for things that we are privileged to have, one of which was books. <br /><br /></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_37606531" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/37606531" />
    <a title="Michelle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1118583-michelle"><img alt="Michelle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1234844345p2/1118583.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37606531?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michelle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michelle" href="/user/show/1118583-michelle">Michelle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780061130243" href="/book/show/51639.Black_Boy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37606531" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4748161793496062260">Richard Wright grew up in the woods of Mississippi amid poverty, hunger, fear, and hatred. He lied, stole, and raged at those around him; at six he was a "drunkard," hanging about in taverns. Surly, brutal, cold, suspicious, and self-pitying, he was surrounded on one side by whites who were either indifferent to him, pitying, or cruel, and on the other by blacks who resented anyone trying to rise above the common lot. <br/><br/>Black Boy is Richard Wright's powerful account of his journey from innocence t</span>
  <span id="freeText4748161793496062260" style="display:none">Richard Wright grew up in the woods of Mississippi amid poverty, hunger, fear, and hatred. He lied, stole, and raged at those around him; at six he was a "drunkard," hanging about in taverns. Surly, brutal, cold, suspicious, and self-pitying, he was surrounded on one side by whites who were either indifferent to him, pitying, or cruel, and on the other by blacks who resented anyone trying to rise above the common lot. <br /><br />Black Boy is Richard Wright's powerful account of his journey from innocence to experience in the Jim Crow South. It is at once an unashamed confession and a profound indictment—a poignant and disturbing record of social injustice and human suffering. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4748161793496062260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1610707254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17825885785882888954">This was so vast and widely important and touched on so much. I have never felt so personally connected to a book. Maybe when I’ll grow up I’ll make this into a mini series. That’s my dream.<br/><br/>11/20/20: I'm not as enamored of this as I was in high school. I don't know if I'm reading more closely or if my thoughts have just changed, but I noticed a lot of snide comments toward Black people in general. I was sort of shocked at the page where he called black people "lacking in culture" and "tenderness</span>
  <span id="freeText17825885785882888954" style="display:none">This was so vast and widely important and touched on so much. I have never felt so personally connected to a book. Maybe when I’ll grow up I’ll make this into a mini series. That’s my dream.<br /><br />11/20/20: I'm not as enamored of this as I was in high school. I don't know if I'm reading more closely or if my thoughts have just changed, but I noticed a lot of snide comments toward Black people in general. I was sort of shocked at the page where he called black people "lacking in culture" and "tenderness." It sounded like he hated us. And reading about the way he treated Zora Neale Hurston... I don't think of him as a hero now, but just as human, I think. </span>

    <a title="Samantha Lazar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1602815-samantha-lazar"><img alt="Samantha Lazar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1225405140p2/1602815.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37035158?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samantha Lazar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samantha Lazar" href="/user/show/1602815-samantha-lazar">Samantha Lazar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1602815-samantha-lazar?shelf=informational">informational</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1602815-samantha-lazar?shelf=multicultural-literature">multicultural-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37035158" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer508131273918558787">This book is an autobiography about Richard Wright's life. He grew up in the Jim Crow South in terrible living conditions. He was separated from his mother many times because she could not afford to provide for Richard and his brother, he was physically beaten, and verbally abused because of his race. Some parts of the book move rather slow, but for the most part, I really enjoyed reading it. It is so sad to see how people were treated because of their skin color. Ignorant people need to learn c</span>
  <span id="freeText508131273918558787" style="display:none">This book is an autobiography about Richard Wright's life. He grew up in the Jim Crow South in terrible living conditions. He was separated from his mother many times because she could not afford to provide for Richard and his brother, he was physically beaten, and verbally abused because of his race. Some parts of the book move rather slow, but for the most part, I really enjoyed reading it. It is so sad to see how people were treated because of their skin color. Ignorant people need to learn compassion, acceptance, and the ability to appreciate others differences.</span>
  <a data-text-id="508131273918558787" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8kfo3Lw7tT0/FSYEP2j1uU83NmuiOfi2QvB8Arh7HTTIYYEOB2V8VZg+kmXInf9mmWjRo2y0dtEZjjqzdTjvrg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('H6xk54kLMEKhwUyma51RNX3TurjLCpvISgdiwHD3Wzclig01MlX5Kgbq+MecaFvqq4xdcAWHFa8ReSRxvbSprQ==')})
  }

Du Bois (1868–1963) played a key role in developing the strategy and program that dominated early 20th-century black protest in America. In this collection of essays, first published together in 1903, he eloquently affirms that it is beneath the dignity of a human being to beg for those rights that belong inherently to all mankind. He also charges that the strategy of accommodation to white supremacy advanced by Booker T. Washington, then the most influential black leader in America, would only serve to perpetuate black oppression.\nPublication of The Souls of Black Folk was a dramatic event that helped to polarize black leaders into two groups: the more conservative followers of Washington and the more radical supporters of aggressive protest. Its influence cannot be overstated. It is essential reading for everyone interested in African-American history and the struggle for civil rights in America.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13887627886017340704\" href=\"#\" <span id="freeTextContainer8465583010217121782">Richard Nathaniel Wright was an African-American author of powerful, sometimes controversial novels, short stories and non-fiction. Much of his literature concerned racial themes. His work helped redefine discussions of race relations in America in the mid-20th century.<br/><br/><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/44217-master-list-of-author-disambiguation-by-spaces' rel='nofollow noopener'>See this thread for more information.</a></i> </span>
